        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6897777" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer373378339702673964">Before Goodreads, before the Internet (aka the dark ages) I kept a list of Books Read and I've finally added them all in here. On that list is Our Mutual Friend. The title is right there, in my handwriting. So I must have read it. As it is 900 pages long, you would think I'd remember it, but I don't. In fact I had thought it was the one remaining Big Dickens I hadn't read & was saving it for a rainy day, or 90 rainy days. Now I am wondering if I was possibly not sober when I added it to my Books</span>
  <span id="freeText373378339702673964" style="display:none">Before Goodreads, before the Internet (aka the dark ages) I kept a list of Books Read and I've finally added them all in here. On that list is Our Mutual Friend. The title is right there, in my handwriting. So I must have read it. As it is 900 pages long, you would think I'd remember it, but I don't. In fact I had thought it was the one remaining Big Dickens I hadn't read &amp; was saving it for a rainy day, or 90 rainy days. Now I am wondering if I was possibly not sober when I added it to my Books Read list, and I did that just to mess with my future self's mind, i.e. me now, and make me think I have incipient Alzheimers. Why would I do that? Why would I fall out with my future self? What did the present me do to the past me? Or maybe it's because my brain is now full, and in order to make room for a new fact I have to forget an old fact. If so I'm glad I forgot something as trifling as reading a 900 page book, rather than say my mother's address or the name of the company I work for. <br /> The five star rating is purely sentimental, Dickens was a genius.</span>

<div id="review_1361948060" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1361948060" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1361948060?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=gutenberg-download">gutenberg-download</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1361948060" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5282308453131732211">If you have ever read Charles Dickens, you will know that his plot lines, characters, and literary devices are myriad, and for my thinking, Our Mutual Friend might employ more of those than any other of his novels that I have read. In the beginning, this made the thread a little harder to keep untangled, but in the end, it served his purposes beautifully.<br/><br/>There are, for your entertainment, two major love stories, a mysterious imposter, a murderer or two, a few men of nefarious occupation, a coupl</span>
  <span id="freeText5282308453131732211" style="display:none">If you have ever read Charles Dickens, you will know that his plot lines, characters, and literary devices are myriad, and for my thinking, Our Mutual Friend might employ more of those than any other of his novels that I have read. In the beginning, this made the thread a little harder to keep untangled, but in the end, it served his purposes beautifully.<br /><br />There are, for your entertainment, two major love stories, a mysterious imposter, a murderer or two, a few men of nefarious occupation, a couple of red herrings and several mistreated, but eternally good, individuals. Jenny Wren is a marvelous character, along with the Jew, Riah, who helps to atone for the evil character of Fagin in Oliver Twist. Betty Higden is a superb example of the worthy poor, and the Boffins are an unforgettable couple. I was particularly interested in Lizzie Hexam and Eugene Wrayburn, a part of the plot that was less easy to predict than some of the others. Both the love stories are captivating, and the ins and outs, and coincidental crossings, of each of the characters with the others is masterful. This is a later work, and the maturity of the writing and plot control are obvious.<br /><br />Then there is just the irrefutable wisdom of Mr. Dickens:<br /><i>And this is another spell against which the shedder of blood for ever strives in vain. There are fifty doors by which discovery may enter. With infinite pains and cunning, he double locks and bars forty-nine of them, and cannot see the fiftieth standing wide open.</i><br /><br />Ah, Mr. Dickens, may it ever be so!<br /><br />Not an unusual subject for Dickens, he deals with the plight of the poor and the inadequate methods of alleviating it, and he does it with deftness and just the right touch of sentiment.<br /><i>For when we have got things to the pass that with an enormous treasure at disposal to relieve the poor, the best of the poor detest our mercies, hide their heads from us, and shame us by starving to death in the midst of us, it is a pass impossible of prosperity, impossible of continuance. It may not be so written in the Gospel according to Podsnappery, you may not find these words for the text of a sermon, in the Returns of the Board of Trade; but they have been the truth since the foundations of the universe were laid, and they will be the truth until the foundations of the universe are shaken by the Builder.</i><br /><br />Does our modern society not still wrestle with how to help people pull themselves up without damaging their worth in their own eyes? Do we not still have a system that creates a class barrier and with the very assistance we offer sometimes assure that people will remain and always be aware that their class is not “our” class?<br /><br />There are almost as many themes as there are characters. Money, its influence and its corrupting properties, is one, but as the Bible tells us it is the “love of money” that is “the root of all evil” and Dickens makes it clear that it is the fault in the people and not the wealth itself that is objectionable. There is the major theme of class division and the insensibility of choices made for no other reason than that a person is part of one class or the other. There is the significance of friendship and loyalty, the importance of truth and ethics, and the value of trust in relationships, including but not limited to marriage. There is betrayal, but there is also steadfastness and a desire on the part of so many of these characters to overcome the baseness of their worlds and rise above their conditions morally.<br /><br />There were a few sections that plodded, but for the most part I was feeling sorry for the original audience who were forced to wait for the next installment to find out what was to happen and could not just plow ahead, as I found myself inclined to do.<br /><br />The novel is quite long at over 800 pages, but I read over a three month span and enjoyed it immensely. I am making some progress toward my goal of reading ALL of Dickens’ works. Next up is Nicholas Nickleby, and if it is as pleasing for me as this one, I will be quite happy indeed.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5282308453131732211" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_442403338" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/442403338?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MJ Nicholls" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls">MJ Nicholls</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199536252" href="/book/show/3790209-our-mutual-friend">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=oxford-classics">oxford-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=pre-1900s">pre-1900s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=voluminous">voluminous</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=sassysassenachs">sassysassenachs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer442403338" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10268746547274487520">Better to read Dickens in week-long rushes—serialised readers, without the aid of Wiki or plot recaps, will have to summon the heroic powers of recall commonly the resource of Victorian bookworms. How torturous to be put on tenterhooks for months as to John Rokesmith’s identity enigma, to think of the vagabond Wegg ruining the sweet old Mr Boffin. Perhaps now, at the end of my Monster Dickens reading, it is pertinent to ask of these novels—page-turners of their day, morally instructional enterta</span>
  <span id="freeText10268746547274487520" style="display:none">Better to read Dickens in week-long rushes—serialised readers, without the aid of Wiki or plot recaps, will have to summon the heroic powers of recall commonly the resource of Victorian bookworms. How torturous to be put on tenterhooks for months as to John Rokesmith’s identity enigma, to think of the vagabond Wegg ruining the sweet old Mr Boffin. Perhaps now, at the end of my Monster Dickens reading, it is pertinent to ask of these novels—page-turners of their day, morally instructional entertainment, or works of art? Answer: all three and more. These are omninovels that defy snubbing. In his last completed work before a long novel-wards sabbatical, Dickens once more chips away at an old theme: the corruption of money, how it seeps into society, and poisons everything. Not that Chaz was a raging anticapitalist, quite the opposite, but there’s no point in being a millionaire if you behave like a spoilt child hoarding all the sweets. In an age badly in need of strong moral fiction (hurts me to say, but tis true), this message still needs to be drilled into the heads of the moneyspinners of the free world. <i>Our Mutual Friend</i> is a brilliant (complete) swansong from Chaz, full of collectively captivating plots and subplots, and some more complex personnel than usual (Wrayburn and Headstone) and your usual vivid, striking and compassionate prose mastery. Farewell, big Chaz! </span>
  <a data-text-id="10268746547274487520" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2325654490" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2325654490" />
    <a title="Rebecca McNutt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt"><img alt="Rebecca McNutt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602390057p2/36977686.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2325654490?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca McNutt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca McNutt" href="/user/show/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt">Rebecca McNutt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=classic">classic</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2325654490" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5607131998222509970">Money has a way of making excellent fiction all the more powerful and dire. At first I didn't really understand <i>Our Mutual Friend</i> and even thought it was a little boring personally, but it's the kind of story that you can't put down and it gets much better as it goes. It has so many themes but its strong suit is that it can switch from funny to somber in a sudden instant, keeping it engaging and surprising 'till the end.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1688578996" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1688578996" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1688578996?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1688578996" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13945649824471316196"><i>“No one who can read, ever looks at a book, even unopened on a shelf, like one who cannot.”</i><br/><br/>I have certainly been looking at <i>Our Mutual Friend</i> on my TBR shelf for years. He kept shaking my fist at it, muttering “One day, damn you! One day!” <br/><br/>Started July 5th, finished August 20th, that is almost two months. It took so long because it is over 800 pages in length, and I read it mostly it in audiobook format. On my commutes to work, which means no progress most weekends. Towards the end of the book, </span>
  <span id="freeText13945649824471316196" style="display:none"><i>“No one who can read, ever looks at a book, even unopened on a shelf, like one who cannot.”</i><br><br>I have certainly been looking at <i>Our Mutual Friend</i> on my TBR shelf for years. He kept shaking my fist at it, muttering “One day, damn you! One day!” <br><br>Started July 5th, finished August 20th, that is almost two months. It took so long because it is over 800 pages in length, and I read it mostly it in audiobook format. On my commutes to work, which means no progress most weekends. Towards the end of the book, I also started playing Pokemon Go on the bus so that caused further delays.<br><br><i>Our Mutual Friend</i> is epic in length but smallish in scale. Unlike most of Dickens’ novels, it does not—for the most part—have an obvious protagonist but several central characters that the narrative switches between. That is, until the last third of the book that I realized it is John Rokesmith’s story more than anybody else’s. I suppose the fact that he is the eponymous “mutual friend”—as indicated by Mr. Boffin early in the book—should have clued me in!<br><br>John Rokesmith is the secretary of Mr. Boffin, a former servant of a rich miser who inherited his master’s wealth upon the man’s death, his only son having apparently drowned en route back from overseas. Mr. Boffin starts off the book as an extremely amiable chappie, one of Dickens’ colorful, comical and vivid characters.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1471713020i/20097096._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Noddy Boffin<br><br>Unfortunately, he is soon to be seen completely corrupted by wealth and—in an amazing 180 ° personality shift of “Jekyll &amp; Hyde” proportions—becomes something of a beastly scumbag*. His secretary, on the other hand, is the novel’s moral centre and becomes the brunt of beastly Boffin’s abuses and accusations.<br><br>In contrast to Mr. Boffin, another potential protagonist Lizzie Hexam, appears to be completely incorruptible and not in the least tempted by wealth if the opportunity seems to go against her moral conviction. Lizzie is one of Dickens’ stock impossibly angelic female characters, but damn if he doesn’t always manage to make them all quite lovable in spite of being too good to be true.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1471713020i/20097097._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Lizzie Hexam<br><br>The plotline of <i>Our Mutual Friend</i> is not possible to outline succinctly, it is quite densely plotted, full of mysteries, twists and turns; not to mention laughs. The central theme, however, is the effect of wealth. How it can corrupt even decent people; but also how it can not corrupt people with moral fortitude. A similar theme is the effect of love, depending on whether love is selfish or selfless it can also corrupt or redeem. Bradley Headstone is introduced to the readers as a hard working schoolmaster but later on an unrequited love turns him into a scheming psychopath. On the other hand, Mr. Eugene Wrayburn, a handsome, roguish, insolent (not to mention uppity!) young barrister, is redeemed by the love of a good woman.<br><br>If all this sounds terribly serious <i>Our Mutual Friend</i> is actually often very funny. One of the reasons I often return to read Dickens is that he always populates his books with colorful, eccentric and often hilarious characters. Top prize for the most colorful character in this book goes to Jenny Wren, a dolls’ dressmaker. She is crippled with a bad back, and walks with the aid of a stick but is always of a sunny disposition and treats practically every man she meets like an imbecile. She is also one of the book’s most perceptive characters and nobody seems to be able to the better of her.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1471713020i/20097098._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Jenny Wren<br><br>Also very memorable is the aforementioned Boffin, whether he is good or evil, he is never less than entertaining. There is also the even more villainous Silas Wegg, ballad merchant extraordinaire, with a wooden leg, and “Fascination Fledgeby” who really lives up to his name; a man with any redeemable quality but is still almost sympathetic because he does get the stuffing beaten out of him and almost literally gets salt rubbed in his wounds afterward. There are numerous other interesting characters, but I don’t want to spend all day writing this review, and you would have much more fun discovering them for yourself.<br><br>At more than 800 pages I would say Dickens-once again-overwrote. However, as he published his novels in monthly installments in magazines the length is understandable. Also, unlike <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13661.Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo" rel="nofollow noopener"> Victor Hugo</a>’s catatonia-inducing expositions about the Paris sewage system and whatnot in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24280.Les_Mis_rables" title="Les Misérables by Victor Hugo" rel="nofollow noopener"> Les Misérables</a>, Dickens does nor over indulge with the “TMI” expositions. <i>Our Mutual Friend</i> is always readable and I never felt any urge to skip even one paragraph. <br><br>I have recommended every Dickens’ book I review, this one is no different. It may not be as much fun as Pokemon Go but probably more rewarding<br>_______________<br><b>Notes:</b><br>* Boffin’s sudden 180° moral change did initially seem more like a plot device than a realistic character development, but it eventually makes sense, and even kind of hilarious.<br><br>• Audiobook credit: Another excellent professional-level <a href="https://librivox.org/our-mutual-friend-version-3-by-charles-dickens/" rel="nofollow noopener">narration by Mil Nicholson</a>. Thank you!<br><br>• Thank you Shmoop for their <a href="http://www.shmoop.com/our-mutual-friend/" rel="nofollow noopener">helpful notes</a>, though <i>Our Mutual Friend</i> is not as hard to read as they mention; no harder than any of the Dickens books I read so far.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13945649824471316196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText148963967866176102" style="display:none">[italiano sotto]<br /><br />So far, my favourite among Dickens’s books; it made me want to read them all (in order of writing, why not?).<br />It makes you laugh, it makes you think, it makes you move. And it makes you wonder. And it makes you admire.<br />And it disorientates you.<br /><br />From halfway on, you are less disoriented. But in the meantime you have come to love Mr and Mrs Boffin.<br /><br />Then the central theme seems to become the corruption - or the risk of corruption, the fear and the charm of corruption - that money brings with it. So Mr Boffin becomes a monster, but...<br /><br />[I have tagged it <i>città</i>, cities: and the city is London, of course. With an unforgettable beginning at night on the Thames, and a lot of remarkable walks through the city, and a central importance, as already in <i>A Tale of Two Cities</i>, for the Inns of Court area].<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />Tra quelli che ho letto finora, il mio Dickens preferito. Anzi, a dirla tutta, è quello che mi ha fatto venire la folle idea di leggerli tutti, a partire dall'inizio.<br /><br />Si ride, si pensa, ci si commuove, si ammira, ci si domanda.<br /><br />Ah, e ci si disorienta, anche.<br /><br />Arrivati circa a metà, ci si disorienta meno. Ma intanto si è sviluppata una certa passioncella per Mr. and Mrs. Boffin.<br /><br />Dopodiché il tema centrale sembra diventare la corruzione - o il rischio della corruzione, il timore e il fascino della corruzione - che il denaro porta con sé.<br />E quindi Mr. Boffin diventa un mostro, ma...<br /><br />[Città: Londra, ovviamente. Memorabile l'inizio notturno sul Tamigi].<br /><br />(Ho letto anche una traduzione italiana che normalizza un po' troppo lo stile di Dickens, appianando le ridondanze, le ripetizioni ironiche, ecc. Mi era già capitato con una traduzione di <i>Persuasion</i> di Jane Austen: letto in italiano sembrava un esempio classico della costruzione inglese secca e diretta ma in confronto l'originale sembrava Proust, con molte subordinate, frasi complesse, incastonate una nell'altra...<br /><br />Misteri).</span>

<div id="review_62016246" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/62016246" />
    <a title="Grace Tjan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1889855-grace-tjan"><img alt="Grace Tjan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307595874p2/1889855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/62016246?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Grace Tjan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Grace Tjan" href="/user/show/1889855-grace-tjan">Grace Tjan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781853261947" href="/book/show/844708.Our_Mutual_Friend">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1889855-grace-tjan?shelf=2010">2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1889855-grace-tjan?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1889855-grace-tjan?shelf=victorian-lit">victorian-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1889855-grace-tjan?shelf=british-literature">british-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1889855-grace-tjan?shelf=swapable">swapable</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1889855-grace-tjan?shelf=dickens">dickens</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer62016246" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1075555808112942607">3.5 stars<br/><br/>SPOILERS!<br/><br/><br/>What I learned from this book (in no particular order):<br/><br/>1.	You can use the same adjective 19 times in a short chapter to describe a single character and still be considered a great literary stylist. Yes, I get it, Mr. Dickens: Bella’s adorable father is CHERUBIC.<br/><br/>2.	It is perfectly acceptable to deceive your wife-to-be, and even marry her under an assumed identity, for the noble purpose of ascertaining her moral worthiness. <br/><br/>3.	Once you are convinced that she is no gold-digger, sh</span>
  <span id="freeText1075555808112942607" style="display:none">3.5 stars<br /><br />SPOILERS!<br /><br /><br />What I learned from this book (in no particular order):<br /><br />1.	You can use the same adjective 19 times in a short chapter to describe a single character and still be considered a great literary stylist. Yes, I get it, Mr. Dickens: Bella’s adorable father is CHERUBIC.<br /><br />2.	It is perfectly acceptable to deceive your wife-to-be, and even marry her under an assumed identity, for the noble purpose of ascertaining her moral worthiness. <br /><br />3.	Once you are convinced that she is no gold-digger, she can be informed of your true identity as the sole heir of a wealthy garbage man.<br /><br />4.	She of course, having been established as a person of high moral standing, would take the news with perfect equanimity, even though she was of the mercenary persuasion just before she agreed to marry you.<br /><br />5.	It is perfectly possible for a hard-nosed, mercenary beauty to be reformed through the example of others whose characters have been debased by the sudden acquirement of wealth. <br /><br />6.	A barely literate, retired garbage man with no acting experience whatsoever can convincingly act this example.<br /><br />7.	The notion of the bee as a paragon of industriousness is vastly overrated. We as a bipeds should object on principle to being constantly referred to insects and other four footed creatures. As human beings, we cannot be required to model our behavior on the behaviors of the bee, the dog, the spider or the camel. <br /><br />8.	One of the most salient reasons of why this is so, is the undeniable fact that a camel has several stomachs to entertain himself with, while we poor humans have only one.<br /><br />9.	One of the best ways to educate oneself is to listen to The Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire being read by a one-legged street ballad seller. Thus we may learn of fascinating historical characters such as Polly Beeious (a Roman virgin, and therefore cannot be discussed in polite company), Commodious (an Emperor who is unworthy of his English origins) and Bully Sawyers, a.k.a Belisarius, a great military leader.<br /><br />10.	If you need to have your leg amputated, you can always sell it to Mr. Venus, a bone man whose collection includes preserved Hindu, African and (articulated) English babies, a French Gentleman, human bones (“warious”), mummified birds and dried cuticles. <br /><br />BUT SERIOUSLY,<br /><br />The most entertaining part of the book for me is when Dickens is being caustically funny. Mr. Boffin’s reading of the Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire, Mr. Venus’ dry recitation of his macabre inventory, and Wrayburn’s argument against the bee made me chuckle. The social satire with the social-climbing, money-obsessed Veneerings, Podsnaps et al is piquant and sharp, and perhaps as relevant today as it was in the Victorian era. The plot is intricate but deftly woven, with hardly any improbable coincidences that mar his other works such as A Tale of Two Cities. The evocation of the Thames and the marginal characters that make their living from its ebb and flow is immediate and pungent: we can palpably see and smell the great river, the seaman’s taverns and the muddy lanes where the Hexams and Riderhoods live. The river is a metaphor for growth and decay, and the most interesting characters are those that are associated with it. In fact, I find the supporting cast more interesting than the bland main characters. I don’t really understand who Wrayburn and Rokesmith/Harmon are, aside from the traits that they are given to support their roles in the plot. Bradley Headstone is a one-dimensional plot device. Bella is given more personality than the usual saintly, long-suffering Dickens heroine, but her sudden transformation seems to be hardly credible, and so is her romance with Rokesmith/Harmon. The contrast between the dark satire and the fairy-tale conclusion is jarring, and at times the pace of the story is as slow as the silt-burdened current of the Thames. And I was sorely tempted to fling the book to the wall every time Dickens calls Bella’s father a ‘cherub’ --- it’s like a literary Tourette syndrome. <br /><br />A mixed bag for me, and if not for the melodramatic A plot and bland main characters, should have been a solid four stars.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1075555808112942607" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_273186959" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/273186959" />
    <a title="Lance Greenfield" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2793431-lance-greenfield"><img alt="Lance Greenfield" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442658682p2/2793431.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/273186959?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lance Greenfield" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lance Greenfield" href="/user/show/2793431-lance-greenfield">Lance Greenfield</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2793431-lance-greenfield?shelf=tbr-again">tbr-again</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer273186959" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13102306598740341804">I first read <i>Our Mutual Friend</i> when I was thirteen years old, and I awarded it five stars on Goodreads based on my memory of that first read. I always remembered this as my favourite Charles Dickens novel, and I am still strongly of that opinion. If I could award it yet another five stars, I would. This is a classic masterpiece.<br/><br/>Yards of literary analysis has been written about this book over the decades, and I could not possibly compete with those who have written before me. After all, English L</span>
  <span id="freeText13102306598740341804" style="display:none">I first read <i>Our Mutual Friend</i> when I was thirteen years old, and I awarded it five stars on Goodreads based on my memory of that first read. I always remembered this as my favourite Charles Dickens novel, and I am still strongly of that opinion. If I could award it yet another five stars, I would. This is a classic masterpiece.<br /><br />Yards of literary analysis has been written about this book over the decades, and I could not possibly compete with those who have written before me. After all, English Literature was the only GCE O level that I failed. O levels are the exams that we usually take in UK at the age of sixteen. <br /><br />Instead, I'll just tell you what I think from my own heart and head.<br /><br />First of all, the wonderful use of the English language employed by Dickens, and the extent of vocabulary, just amazed me and is a lesson for all of us.<br /><br />Secondly, the character building of all of the characters, and their development throughout the story, is so strong that one can visualise them all, and start to imagine how they are all thinking and interacting. It is very difficult to understand who the leading characters are, as it seems that there are more than a dozen principals. I think that this is great.<br /><br />Then I would say that everyone should read this book at least twice. Knowing the conclusions does not spoil the reading. I had forgotten much, but I knew roughly where we were going, even though it is over four decades since I first read it. In fact, the second reading is better than the first, because I understood more of why the conclusions were reached as I staggered my way through. I shall definitely read <i>Our Mutual Friend</i> again before long.<br /><br />The complex, interwoven plots are marvellous, and I highly recommend this book to anyone and everyone. It is a very thick volume, agreed, but every word is a gem in a treasure trove of jewels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13102306598740341804" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review273186959">

For some reason, that helped one of the members who had been struggling greatly with the novel.<br/><br/>I struggled with a way to review this complicated novel, as any way I thought of would contain spoilers, especially my thoughts on why Lizzie Hexam is a character that has ‘legs’.<br/><br/>So, I will only say:<br/><br/>When I first read this however many years ago,</span>
  <span id="freeText13164179346107474252" style="display:none">A <b>reread</b> with the Dickens Fellowship of New Orleans: At one of our meetings a speaker said this was not only Dickens’ most cynical work, but also a fairy-tale. For some reason, that helped one of the members who had been struggling greatly with the novel.<br /><br />I struggled with a way to review this complicated novel, as any way I thought of would contain spoilers, especially my thoughts on why Lizzie Hexam is a character that has ‘legs’.<br /><br />So, I will only say:<br /><br />When I first read this however many years ago, I had sympathy for Bradley Headstone; this time I had none. <br /><br />I was supremely aggravated at Bella’s reaction when she’s <b>finally</b> told the truth. I’m pretty sure I felt the same way the first time I read it.<br /><br />If you thought Dickens had skewered Society in his other novels, 'you ain't seen nothing yet.'<br /><br />Jenny Wren remains my favorite character: <i>"Don't be long gone. Come back, and be dead!" </i></span>

<div id="review_29040325" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/29040325" />
    <a title="R." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/184928-r"><img alt="R." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509742903p2/184928.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/29040325?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="R." class="user" itemprop="url" name="R." href="/user/show/184928-r">R.</a>
      </span>

          marked it as to-read




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer29040325" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14328947551625976865">I don't know if I was supertired or Dickens gawt slawppy, but I spent three pages last night thinking I was reading about the <i>inner life of a dinner table</i> the family had nicknamed "Twemlow".<br/><br/>The confusing to passage: <b>There was an innocent piece of dinner-furniture that went upon easy castors and was kept over a livery stable-yard in Duke Street, Saint James's, when not in use, to whom the Veneerings were a source of blind confusion. The name of this article was Twemlow. Being first cousin to Lord</b></span>
  <span id="freeText14328947551625976865" style="display:none">I don't know if I was supertired or Dickens gawt slawppy, but I spent three pages last night thinking I was reading about the <i>inner life of a dinner table</i> the family had nicknamed "Twemlow".<br /><br />The confusing to passage: <b>There was an innocent piece of dinner-furniture that went upon easy castors and was kept over a livery stable-yard in Duke Street, Saint James's, when not in use, to whom the Veneerings were a source of blind confusion. The name of this article was Twemlow. Being first cousin to Lord Snigsworth, he was in frequent requisition, and at many houses might be said to represent the dining-table in its normal state. Mr and Mrs Veneering, for example, arranging a dinner, habitually started with Twemlow, and then put leaves in him, or added guests to him. Sometimes, the table consisted of Twemlow and half a dozen leaves; sometimes, of Twemlow and a dozen leaves; sometimes, Twemlow was pulled out to his utmost extent of twenty leaves. Mr and Mrs Veneering on occasions of ceremony faced each other in the centre of the board, and thus the parallel still held; for, it always happened that the more Twemlow was pulled out, the further he found himself from the center, and nearer to the sideboard at one end of the room, or the window-curtains at the other.</b><br /><br />Paid by the word much? Bonus for semicolons? Anyways, Dickens intentions are clearer today: he wanted to make certain the Constant Reader got the seating chart for the Veneerings down pat. Thanks, Chuck. Thanks. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14328947551625976865" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2568132191" />

        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/80247437-thomas">Thomas</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2568132191" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer543394747640490266">Again with a Dickens book, I’m reminded of a song:<br/><br/>Money, money, money<br/>Must be funny<br/>In the rich man's world<br/>Money, money, money<br/>Always sunny<br/>In the rich man's world<br/>Aha aha<br/>All the things I could do<br/>If I had a little money<br/>It's a rich man's world<br/>It's a rich man's world<br/><br/>Money, Money, Money – Abba<br/><br/>Can’t you just picture Bella, if she lived in the 1970s, singing that song with gusto?<br/><br/>So I sit at the end of a very long journey, not just this particular long journey (it’s a big book) but through the worlds of Ch</span>
  <span id="freeText543394747640490266" style="display:none">Again with a Dickens book, I’m reminded of a song:<br /><br />Money, money, money<br />Must be funny<br />In the rich man's world<br />Money, money, money<br />Always sunny<br />In the rich man's world<br />Aha aha<br />All the things I could do<br />If I had a little money<br />It's a rich man's world<br />It's a rich man's world<br /><br />Money, Money, Money – Abba<br /><br />Can’t you just picture Bella, if she lived in the 1970s, singing that song with gusto?<br /><br />So I sit at the end of a very long journey, not just this particular long journey (it’s a big book) but through the worlds of Charles Dickens. This is the last of his complete novels and never again will I be able to begin one of these books without having any knowledge of what lies over the next hill or around the next bend. It’s bittersweet.<br /><br />The book itself is an incredibly complex tapestry of subplots and characters.<br /><br />I suppose it’s going to be raining spoilers soon so I might as well put the checkpoint here. Please remove all metal objects including your belt and shoes and put them in the tray before stepping through the metal detector. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br /><br />John Harmon aka Julius Handford aka John Rokesmith is, if anyone is, at the center of the story. I imagine discovering your officially dead, and then deciding to run with it, was a much more original idea when Charles wrote it. I thought it was interesting how quickly our fine author spilled the beans to the reader. I’m sure many would have guessed who Rokesmith really was long before the big reveal, but he didn’t even try. I don’t think it really detracted from the book, it just seemed like a usual choice. I liked John. I was rooting for him.<br /><br />The big reveal, if there was one, involved Mister and Mrs. Boffin having discovered John’s true identity, became involved in a long and complex fake-out pretending that Mister Boffin had been corrupted by his wealth in order to reveal Bella’s true character. This was meant also to teach Bella a lesson and had the side benefit of continuing to string the evil Silas Wegg along until the satisfying point when he gets his final comeuppance. The idea that innocent, unsophisticated Noddy Boffin could have convincingly pulled off such an act for an extended period of time seems the least realistic part of the story. Still, relentless realism is not what I’m looking for in a Charles Dickens novel. I read nonfiction for that.<br /><br />In the end, the long and twisted journey resulting in the marriage of John and Bella is probably the central plot in this story.<br /><br />In my opinion, the second most important plot involves the long and twisted journey resulting in the marriage of Lizzie Hexam and Eugene Wrayburn.<br /><br />I suppose Lizzie would not have been considered a very interesting character by readers who love complexity. Lizzie is honest and decent to the core. The sort of noncriminal poor person we never hear about because they never draw attention to themselves. They’re much more common than many people think. I don’t care if she wasn’t complex, I liked her and was rooting for her to be happy.<br /><br />Eugene Wrayburn, like John Harmon, was reborn by almost drowning. I detect a watery theme here. Actually there are a lot of watery themes in this book as much of it takes place along the shores of a river. Rivers tend to be pretty wet. Eugene becomes a better person through his run-in with one of the more complex and truly screwed up of all of Dickens villains.<br /><br />Bradley Headstone, now there’s a complex character. He’s messed up on so many levels that it’s hard to keep track of them all. Lizzie didn’t simply reject him because she didn’t love him. When Headstone proposes to her, it’s one of the creepiest things in the book. He might as well been saying, “Love me or else.” That’s not a lover, that’s a stocker. The fact that he stocked Eugene and not Lizzie was really just a detail. I guess he was just an old-fashioned guy, any self-respecting modern stocker would’ve gone after the girl. When he, along with the evil "Rogue" Riderhood drowned together, it was one of those satisfying “two birds with one stone” moments.<br /><br />I found myself wishing Mr. Venus and Pleasant Riderhood a long and happy life together.<br /><br />The Lammles are hilarious, two con-artists who conned each other. As they rode off into the sunset, more like the sunrise considering the direction they were heading, I couldn’t help but feel that if something ever happened to Alfred, Sophronia might actually be redeemable.<br /><br />When I think about Mr. Riah, I find myself wondering if the Jewish community accepted the apology for Fagin he represented.<br /><br />Jenny Wren is one of the more wonderful and original characters Charles Dickens ever came up with. I’m glad to have met the little doll’s dressmaker and I found myself hoping that she and Sloppy might find happiness together, or at least friendship.<br /><br />Georgiana Podsnap needed someone to cherish and protect her. She felt to me like someone who would thrive in those circumstances.<br /><br />Mr. and Mrs. Podsnap were the very epitome of the depths and shallows of Podsnappery.<br /><br />The Veneerings had a very nice shiny veneer, very admirable, I’m sure, or so says the Voice of Society. I wonder if Lady Tippins is that voice?<br /><br />One of my favorite passages from this book involves Lady Tippins. It goes as follows:<br /><br />"Betimes next morning, that horrible old Lady Tippins (relict of the late Sir Thomas Tippins, knighted in mistake for somebody else by His Majesty King George the Third, who, while performing the ceremony, was graciously pleased to observe, 'What, what, what? Who, who, who? Why, why, why?') begins to be dyed and varnished for the interesting occasion. She has a reputation for giving smart accounts of things, and she must be at these people's early, my dear, to lose nothing of the fun. Whereabout in the bonnet and drapery announced by her name, any fragment of the real woman may be concealed, is perhaps known to her maid; but you could easily buy all you see of her, in Bond Street; or you might scalp her, and peel her, and scrape her, and make two Lady Tippinses out of her, and yet not penetrate to the genuine article. She has a large gold eye-glass, has Lady Tippins, to survey the proceedings with. If she had one in each eye, it might keep that other drooping lid up, and look more uniform. But perennial youth is in her artificial flowers, and her list of lovers is full."<br /><br />I felt bad for Miss Peecher. It must have been devastating for her.<br /><br />I found myself not caring about what happened to Charley Hexam after he separated himself from Headstone. I’m sure he ended up being just as respectable as he planned. I have no doubt that Lizzie cared.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />All in all, it was another fine journey through the Dickensian realms. Fascinating, eccentric characters galore, complex twisting plots that take you all over the place, showing you the arrogance of the middle-class and the plight of the poor. It was a fitting end to my journey through the novels of Charles Dickens. I feel like I’m a better man for it.<br /><br />So I now must say farewell to Oliver Twist, The Artful Dodger, Nancy, Nicholas Nickleby, Smike, Little Nell, Barnaby Rudge, Tom Pinch, Mark Tapley, Florence Dombey, Susan Nipper, David Copperfield, Clara Peggotty, Esther Summerson, John Jarndyce, Louisa Gradgrind, Arthur Clennam, Amy Dorrit, Lucie Manette, Pip, Joe Gargery, Estella, John Harmon, Bella Wilfer, Lizzie Hexam and to all the other of the vast range of characters both saintly and villainous, not to mention just plain eccentric, but always memorable that populate the Dickensian world. Farewell, at least, until we meet again. I will never again be able to meet you all for the first time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="543394747640490266" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2445938134" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer4034971510740288342">The difference between Austen and Dickens, from Nabokov's lectures on literature....<br/><br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/quotes/948440-we-are-now-ready-to-tackle-dickens-we-are-now'>https://www.goodreads.com/quotes/9484...</a><br/><br/>=========<br/><br/>Our Mutual Friend was Dickens last completed novel (1864-1865) and it was interesting to learn that it was not that popular with readers of the time compared with many of his other novels. But also that it became popular with modern readers.<br/><br/>Certainly the great dust mound and the practice of scavengers dragging drowned bodies out of the Thames to rifle through their</span>
  <span id="freeText4034971510740288342" style="display:none">The difference between Austen and Dickens, from Nabokov's lectures on literature....<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/quotes/948440-we-are-now-ready-to-tackle-dickens-we-are-now">https://www.goodreads.com/quotes/9484...</a><br /><br />=========<br /><br />Our Mutual Friend was Dickens last completed novel (1864-1865) and it was interesting to learn that it was not that popular with readers of the time compared with many of his other novels. But also that it became popular with modern readers.<br /><br />Certainly the great dust mound and the practice of scavengers dragging drowned bodies out of the Thames to rifle through their pockets for valuables seem quite peculiar to the time of Dickens. But the behavior of many of the characters had an eerie familiarity. <br /><br />"The class Dickens belonged to, at least by adoption, was growing suddenly rich after a couple of centuries of obscurity. It had grown up mainly in the big towns, out of contact with agriculture, and politically impotent; government, in its experience, was something which either interfered or persecuted. Consequently it was a class with no tradition of public service and not much tradition of usefulness. What now strikes us as remarkable about the new moneyed class of the nineteenth century is their complete irresponsibility; they see everything in terms of individual success, with hardly any consciousness that the community exists." (Orwell) <br /><br />By now, not only the data but several books depict our era as a new Gilded Age, in which the income gap is as high as what existed during the original Gilded Age after the Civil War. With this, comes not only our own tycoons, corruption in politics and business, but also a similar trickle down effect of greed, materialism and lack of morals among a broader population, just as depicted in the novel. A lot of average people are chasing money today as if it will be their salvation, even if it involves getting involved in scams, as victims or perpetrators.<br /><br />================<br /><br />New article on this novel (spoiler alert)<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.theguardian.com/books/2020/jun/23/reading-group-charles-dickens-our-mutual-friend-fairytale-endings">https://www.theguardian.com/books/202...</a><br /><br />==============<br /><br />Orwell's famous essay on Dickens....<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.orwellfoundation.com/the-orwell-foundation/orwell/essays-and-other-works/charles-dickens/">https://www.orwellfoundation.com/the-...</a></span>

<div id="review_1512471732" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1512471732" />
    <a title="Donna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6217162-donna"><img alt="Donna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430955688p2/6217162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1512471732?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Donna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Donna" href="/user/show/6217162-donna">Donna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6217162-donna?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1512471732" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7074560556684217597">3.5 stars<br/><br/>The way this book started gave me chills. Imagine a dark night in which a young woman is rowing a boat on the Thames, her father, a gruff man, steering the boat as he searches the murky water for drowned bodies that he can rob before tying them to his boat and dragging them to shore to turn them over to the authorities for a fee. The daughter keeps her gaze averted as her father leans over the boat and finally snags a body. He yells at her to watch him, her face a frozen mask as she wat</span>
  <span id="freeText7074560556684217597" style="display:none">3.5 stars<br /><br />The way this book started gave me chills. Imagine a dark night in which a young woman is rowing a boat on the Thames, her father, a gruff man, steering the boat as he searches the murky water for drowned bodies that he can rob before tying them to his boat and dragging them to shore to turn them over to the authorities for a fee. The daughter keeps her gaze averted as her father leans over the boat and finally snags a body. He yells at her to watch him, her face a frozen mask as she watches it all, and not for the first time. What a beginning! <br /><br />So imagine my surprise when nothing much exciting happened for the next 400 pages. I had characters coming out of my ears, talking talking, but I didn't know if or why they might be important or how they related back to the chilling events at the beginning. But this wasn't my first time reading Dickens, so I knew everything must relate and every character must be important when the author finally got around to telling me how. I only needed to be patient enough to wade through all the words to be rewarded. And I was, beginning at the halfway mark and onward when the characters and their doings began falling into place, if not falling all over the place in pursuit of money and the power it would bring them. <br /><br />The characters are the best and most memorable part in any Dickens' novel with all their quirkiness and strange names: the Podsnaps, the Veneerings, Bradley Headstone, Pleasant Riderhood, Jenny Wren, Lizzie Hexam, Noddy Boffin, to name only a few in this book. But this story begins with the murder of a man with the common name of John Harmon. Harmon had come to London to claim his inheritance from his deceased and formerly estranged father, a condition of the will being that he must first marry a beautiful but spoiled young woman he had never met, to claim any riches. But with Harmon murdered, an unassuming employee of Harmon's father was to be next in line to inherit a fortune that might just prove to be his misfortune. <br /><br />Despite this novel being written in 1865, Dickens wrote it in what felt like a very modern style with a shocking beginning, and then twists and turns throughout the story that had me thinking, "No way!" as he brought everything around full circle. But I didn't set out to read this novel for that kind of excitement. I read it to see if Dickens would redeem himself for what seemed a very anti-Semitic tone in Oliver Twist which I never did finish for that reason. I'd heard that he had purposefully created a wonderful Jewish character in this book as an apology to the Jewish community. And he did redeem himself with the character, Riah, but not because of Riah's benevolence. He had his faults and weaknesses like any other man, which became most apparent during an amazing speech he made near the end of the book. He admonished himself for unbecoming behavior that did no credit to himself or others of his religion. He also spoke of the dangers of using any individual as representative of whomever his people were, as an excuse for hatred of an entire people. I wasn't expecting this kind of wisdom in this dark story that had less comic relief than many other novels by this author. This book is about corruption in society as most of his novels were, but also corruption of the soul when people value and pursue the wrong things, harming those in their way. It was to be Dickens last complete novel, which made it particularly sad when he addressed the reader in an afterword in which he admitted he was grateful to have lived to finish this novel having been in a near fatal accident recently. <br /><br />I want to add that this book has strong, admirable female characters, much more so than some other books by Dickens that were dominated by males. In this book, the reader watches many of the women evolve and strive for dignity and control over their own lives as much as possible during those times. And interestingly enough, there are four complex father/daughter relationships helping to drive the story forward to a memorable conclusion. Actually, nearly all the characters in this novel evolved for better or worse into something complex and nuanced in personality and in motivation. <br /><br />If only the first half of this book had been more compelling and less loosely knit, I would have given it four stars for those great characters who wanted the best in life, but not always looking to get it in the best possible way. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7074560556684217597" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1657323812" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1657323812" />
    <a title="Lady Clementina ffinch-ffarowmore" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore"><img alt="Lady Clementina ffinch-ffarowmore" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560079929p2/5917681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1657323812?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lady Clementina ffinch-ffarowmore" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lady Clementina ffinch-ffarowmore" href="/user/show/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore">Lady Clementina ffinch-ffarowmore</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=murder">murder</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=classic-literature">classic-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=dickens">dickens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=social-commentary">social-commentary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=british-literature">british-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1657323812" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9936229785553130739">Yet another revisit—my third at least of this one. Dickens last completed novel tells many intertwined stories, but there are three central threads. We have the old miser Mr Harmon recently deceased, who has left all his wealth to his only son John on condition that he marries Bella Wilfer, a young lady Mr Harmon has ‘picked’ because of her not very pleasant nature. But as our story opens, Gaffer Hexam who makes his living pulling out bodies from the Thames and handing them to the authorities, h</span>
  <span id="freeText9936229785553130739" style="display:none">Yet another revisit—my third at least of this one. Dickens last completed novel tells many intertwined stories, but there are three central threads. We have the old miser Mr Harmon recently deceased, who has left all his wealth to his only son John on condition that he marries Bella Wilfer, a young lady Mr Harmon has ‘picked’ because of her not very pleasant nature. But as our story opens, Gaffer Hexam who makes his living pulling out bodies from the Thames and handing them to the authorities, has found a body, that of John Harmon. The property goes to the Boffins the Harmons’ caretakers, and they invite Bella to come live with them. We also follow the story of Gaffer and his children, Lizzie Hexam his daughter, and Charlie his son who Lizzie is secretly sending to school so that he may have a better life. Harmon’s lawyer Mortmer Lightwood and his friend Eugene meet them because of their connection with the case and Eugene begins to take an interest in Lizzie. The third thread is society, the nouveau-riche Veneerings, the snobbish Podnapps, the pretenders the Lammles. Money as I wrote in my previous review is a central theme here, but there is also a murder mystery, family relationships (siblings, parents and children), love, and society. Society, in terms of its attitudes to wealth and class, dropping and picking up people as they acquire or lose their money, contemptuous of the latter, and yet perhaps everything to keep them there. In Betty Hidgen’s character, Dickens makes a strong statement against the poor law in place at the time, and this If I remember correct played a role in reforms in this area. Through Mr Riah, we critique society’s tendency to condemn entire peoples and communities, refusing to acknowledge that there are ‘good’ and ‘bad’ people in every group. Dickens’ storytelling is wonderful as always, and reading in instalments (as I did this time again) one gets a sense of the excitement the original readers must have felt having to stop at different points, having to wait a week to find out what happened next. In Dickens’ books, for me, besides the stories themselves, it is his characters that make them memorable. This book too has such characters in the form of Betty Hidgen, Mr Riah, Jenny Wren the dolls dressmaker, and Podsnap, to name a few. I enjoyed reading this book very much and look forward to my next revisit! </span>
  <a data-text-id="9936229785553130739" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Z0gCuypTr8xhclQisL8RBk7fk6fEIwSZSOT7DfXhkTVdbmtpkQ1mpMZZ4ENHShvZmIB0bwquiv4Tmr28OKJjrw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2Fpoyubz6s31MStAhnyVWUdHenqNEiLcZ59zYgXev9/ifAEYXa0jpVIanyFxiZ+GkRidskOfrLs84TXTyJ1NRQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

You MUST marry money\'\n\n\nDoctor Thorne, considered by Trollope to be the best of his works, is a telling examination of the relationship between money and morality. \n\nIt recounts the story of the son of a bankrupt landowner, Frank Gresham, who is intent on marrying his beloved Mary Thorne despite her illegitimacy and apparent poverty. Forthright, brusque and driven by a firm desire to educate the working-class, Felix is at first viewed with suspicion by many, including the elegant but vain Esther Lyon, the daughter of the local clergyman. As she discovers, however, his blunt words conceal both passion and deep integrity. Soon the romantic and over-refined Esther finds herself overwhelmed by a heart-wrenching decision: whether to choose the wealthy Transome as a husband, or the impoverished but honest Felix       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens">About Charles Dickens</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1387078070p3/239579.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens">Charles Dickens
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
23,206
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15891684463476112810"><b>Charles John Huffam Dickens</b> was a writer and social critic who created some of the world's best-known fictional characters and is regarded as the greatest novelist of the Victorian era. His works enjoyed unprecedented popularity during his lifetime, and by the twentieth century critics and scholars had recognised him as a literary genius. His novels and short stories enjoy lasting popularity.<br/><br/>Dicke</span>
  <span id="freeText15891684463476112810" style="display:none"><b>Charles John Huffam Dickens</b> was a writer and social critic who created some of the world's best-known fictional characters and is regarded as the greatest novelist of the Victorian era. His works enjoyed unprecedented popularity during his lifetime, and by the twentieth century critics and scholars had recognised him as a literary genius. His novels and short stories enjoy lasting popularity.<br /><br />Dickens left school to work in a factory when his father was incarcerated in a debtors' prison. Despite his lack of formal education, he edited a weekly journal for 20 years, wrote 15 novels, five novellas, hundreds of short stories and non-fiction articles, lectured and performed extensively, was an indefatigable letter writer, and campaigned vigorously for children's rights, education, and other social reforms.<br /><br />Dickens was regarded as the literary colossus of his age. His 1843 novella, <i>A Christmas Carol</i>, remains popular and continues to inspire adaptations in every artistic genre. <i>Oliver Twist</i> and <i>Great Expectations</i> are also frequently adapted, and, like many of his novels, evoke images of early Victorian London. His 1859 novel, <i>A Tale of Two Cities</i>, set in London and Paris, is his best-known work of historical fiction. Dickens's creative genius has been praised by fellow writers—from Leo Tolstoy to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3706.George_Orwell" title="George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">George Orwell</a> and G. K. Chesterton—for its realism, comedy, prose style, unique characterisations, and social criticism. On the other hand, Oscar Wilde, Henry James, and Virginia Woolf complained of a lack of psychological depth, loose writing, and a vein of saccharine sentimentalism. The term Dickensian is used to describe something that is reminiscent of Dickens and his writings, such as poor social conditions or comically repulsive characters.<br /><br />On 8 June 1870, Dickens suffered another stroke at his home after a full day's work on <i>Edwin Drood.</i> He never regained consciousness, and the next day he died at Gad's Hill Place. Contrary to his wish to be buried at Rochester Cathedral "in an inexpensive, unostentatious, and strictly private manner," he was laid to rest in the Poets' Corner of Westminster Abbey. A printed epitaph circulated at the time of the funeral reads: "To the Memory of Charles Dickens (England's most popular author) who died at his residence, Higham, near Rochester, Kent, 9 June 1870, aged 58 years. He was a sympathiser with the poor, the suffering, and the oppressed; and by his death, one of England's greatest writers is lost to the world." His last words were: "On the ground", in response to his sister-in-law Georgina's request that he lie down.<br /><br />(from Wikipedia)</span>
  <a data-text-id="15891684463476112810" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

    <div class="js-dataTooltip" data-use-wtr-tooltip='true'>
        <div class="js-tooltipTrigger tooltipTrigger" data-resource-type='Book' data-resource-id='1953' data-placement='bottom-left'>
